      <p>
       Sources: Hobson,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Toperczer
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nguyenvancoc.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="345" data-file-width="250" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Nguyenvancoc.jpg/200px-Nguyenvancoc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/Nguyenvancoc.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nguyenvancoc.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         President
         <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh" title="Ho Chi Minh">
          Ho Chi Minh
         </a>
         congratulates Captain
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nguyen_Van_Coc" title="Nguyen Van Coc">
          Nguyen Van Coc
         </a>
         , who flew a MiG-21 with the NVAF 921st Fighter Regiment. His 11
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
          <a href="#cite_note-41">
           [N 3]
          </a>
         </sup>
         confirmed air victories made him the highest scoring ace of the Vietnam War
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
          <a href="#cite_note-42">
           [39]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
          <a href="#cite_note-43">
           [40]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The North Vietnamese flew their interceptors with guidance from ground controllers, who positioned the MiGs in ambush battle stations to make their "one pass, then haul ass" attacks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michel_III_42_43_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Michel_III_42_43-44">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The MIGs made fast and often accurate attacks against US formations from several directions (usually the MiG-17s performed head-on attacks and the MiG-21s attacked from the rear). After shooting down a few American planes and forcing some of the F-105s to drop their bombs prematurely, the MiGs did not wait for retaliation, but disengaged rapidly. These "guerrilla warfare in the air" tactics
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michel_III_42_43_44-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Michel_III_42_43-44">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       generally proved successful during the course of the war. In December 1966 the MiG-21 pilots of the 921st FR downed 14 F-105s without any losses.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The U.S. Air Force and the U.S. Navy continued to lay down great expectations on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F4_Phantom" title="F4 Phantom">
        F4 Phantom
       </a>
       , assuming that the massive firepower, the best available on-board radar, the highest speed and acceleration properties, coupled with new tactics, would provide Phantoms with an advantage over the MiGs. But in confrontations with the lighter MiG-21, F-4s began to suffer losses. From May to December 1966, the U.S. air forces lost 47 aircraft, destroying only 12 NVAF fighters in return. From April 1965 to November 1968, over 268 air battles occurred over the skies of North Vietnam. North Vietnam claimed 244 downed U.S. aircraft, while admitting to the loss of 85 MiGs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After a million sorties and nearly 1,000 US aircraft losses,
       <a href="/wiki/Operation_Rolling_Thunder" title="Operation Rolling Thunder">
        Operation Rolling Thunder
       </a>
       came to an end on 1 November 1968.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A poor air-to-air combat
       <a href="/wiki/Loss_exchange_ratio" title="Loss exchange ratio">
        loss-exchange ratios
       </a>
       against the smaller, more agile enemy MiGs during the early part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">
        Vietnam War
       </a>
       eventually led the USN to create their
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navy_Fighter_Weapons_School" title="Navy Fighter Weapons School">
        Navy Fighter Weapons School
       </a>
       , also known as "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TOPGUN" title="TOPGUN">
        TOPGUN
       </a>
       " at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Miramar" title="Naval Air Station Miramar">
        Naval Air Station Miramar
       </a>
       , California on 3 March 1969.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The USAF quickly followed with their own version, titled the
       <a href="/wiki/Dissimilar_air_combat_training" title="Dissimilar air combat training">
        Dissimilar Air Combat Training
       </a>
       (sometimes referred to as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Flag_exercise" title="Red Flag exercise">
        Red Flag
       </a>
       ) program at
       <a href="/wiki/Nellis_Air_Force_Base" title="Nellis Air Force Base">
        Nellis Air Force Base
       </a>
       , Nevada. These two programs employed the subsonic
       <a href="/wiki/Douglas_A-4_Skyhawk" title="Douglas A-4 Skyhawk">
        Douglas A-4 Skyhawk
       </a>
       and the supersonic
       <a href="/wiki/Northrop_F-5" title="Northrop F-5">
        F-5 Tiger II
       </a>
       , as well as the Mach 2.4-capable USAF
       <a href="/wiki/Convair_F-106_Delta_Dart" title="Convair F-106 Delta Dart">
        Convair F-106 Delta Dart
       </a>
       , which mimicked the MiG-21.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The culmination of the struggle in the air in early 1972 was May 10, when the VPAF's aircraft completed 64 sorties, resulting in 15 air battles. VPAF claimed 7
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-4" title="F-4">
        F-4s
       </a>
       were shot down (U.S. confirmed five F-4s were lost
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ). Those, in turn, managed to kill two MiG-21s, three MiG-17s and one MiG-19. May 11, two MiG-21s, who played the role of "bait", brought the four F-4s to 2 MiG-21s circling at low altitude. The MiGs quickly stormed the "Phantoms" and 3 missiles shot down two F-4s. June 13, a MiG-21 unit intercepted a group of F-4s, the second pair of MiGs made a missile attack and were hit by two F-4s. May 18, Vietnamese aircraft made 26 sorties, eight of which resulted in combat, costing four
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-4_Phantoms" title="F-4 Phantoms">
        F-4 Phantoms
       </a>
       while the Vietnamese fighters on that day did not suffer any losses.
      </p>
      <p>
       Over the course of the air war, between 3 April 1965
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 8 January 1973, each side would ultimately claim favorable kill ratios. In 1972 the tally between American and Vietnamese planes stood at 201 air battles. The VPAF lost 54 MiGs (including 36 MiG-21s and one MiG-21US) and they claimed 90 U.S. aircraft were shot down (including 73 F-4 fighters and two spy RF-4C)
      </p>
      <p>
       One MiG-21 was shot down on 21 February 1972 by a U.S. Air Force F-4 Phantom piloted by Major Lodge with 1st Lt Roger Locher as his WSO based at Udorn RTAFB, Thailand. This was claimed to be the first ever U.S. Air Force MiG kill at night, and the first in four years at that time. The intercept occurred near the Fish's Mouth region of the Laos, North Vietnam border.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Two MiG-21s were claimed shot down by
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">
        U.S. Air Force
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-52_Stratofortress" title="Boeing B-52 Stratofortress">
        Boeing B-52 Stratofortress
       </a>
       tail gunners; the only confirmed air-to-air kills made by the B-52. The first aerial victory occurred on 18 December 1972, kill awarded to tail gunner SSgt Samuel Turner, who was awarded the
       <a href="/wiki/Silver_Star" title="Silver Star">
        Silver Star
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The second air-to-air kill took place on 24 December 1972, kill awarded to A1C Albert E. Moore for downing a MiG-21 over the Thai Nguyen railroad yards. Both actions occurred during
       <a href="/wiki/Operation_Linebacker_II" title="Operation Linebacker II">
        Operation Linebacker II
       </a>
       (also known as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christmas_Bombings" title="Christmas Bombings">
        Christmas Bombings
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These air-to-air kills were not confirmed by VPAF.
      </p>
      <p>
       The biggest threat to North Vietnam during the war had always been the
       <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Air_Command" title="Strategic Air Command">
        Strategic Air Command
       </a>
       's B-52 Stratofortress.
       <a href="/wiki/Hanoi" title="Hanoi">
        Hanoi
       </a>
       's MiG-17 and MiG-19
       <a href="/wiki/Interceptor_aircraft" title="Interceptor aircraft">
        interceptors
       </a>
       could not deal with those bombers at their flying altitude. In the summer of 1972 the NVAF was directed to train 12 MiG-21 pilots for the specific mission of attacking and shooting down B-52 bombers; with two-thirds of those pilots specifically trained in the night attack.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 26 December 1972, just two days after tail gunner Albert Moore downed his MiG-21, a
       <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_People%27s_Air_Force" title="Vietnam People's Air Force">
        VPAF
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Vietnamese" title="North Vietnamese">
        North Vietnamese
       </a>
       Air Force) MiG-21MF (number 5121)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       from the 921st
       <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">
        Fighter
       </a>
       Regiment, flown by Major
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pham_Tuan" title="Pham Tuan">
        Phạm Tuân
       </a>
       over Hanoi,
       <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">
        North Vietnam
       </a>
       claimed responsibility for the first aerial combat kill of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Air_Force" title="U.S. Air Force">
        U.S. Air Force
       </a>
       Boeing B-52 Stratofortress in aviation history.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toperczer,_p._66_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Toperczer,_p._66-56">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Stratofortress had been above Hanoi at over 30,000 feet (9,100 m) during Operation Linebacker II, when Major Tuân launched two
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vympel_K-13" title="Vympel K-13">
        Atoll
       </a>
       missiles from 2 kilometres, claiming to have destroyed one of the bombers flying in the three plane formation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toperczer,_p._66_56-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Toperczer,_p._66-56">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other sources argue that his missiles failed to hit their mark, but as he was disengaging, a B-52 from a three-bomber cell in front of his target took a hit from a SAM, exploding in mid-air: this may have caused Tuân to think his missiles destroyed the target he had been aiming for.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Vietnamese side also claims another kill to have taken place on 28 December 1972 by a MiG-21 from the 921st FR, this time flown by Vu Xuan Thieu. Thieu is said to have perished in the explosion of a B-52 hit by his own missiles, having approached the target too closely.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toperczer,_p._67_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Toperczer,_p._67-58">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In this case the Vietnamese version appears to be erroneous: while one MiG-21 kill was claimed by Phantoms that night (this may have been Thieu's MiG), no B-52s were lost to any cause on the date of the claimed kill.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Year-by-year kill claims involving MiG-21s
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-12">
         <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
          [1]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dt>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         1966
        </b>
        : U.S. claimed six MiG-21s destroyed; North Vietnam claimed seven F-4 Phantom IIs and 11 F-105 Thunderchiefs shot down by MiG-21s.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         1967
        </b>
        : U.S. claimed 21 MiG-21s destroyed; North Vietnam claimed 17 F-105 Thunderchiefs, 11 F-4 Phantom IIs, two
        <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_F-101_Voodoo" title="McDonnell F-101 Voodoo">
         McDonnell RF-101 Voodoos
        </a>
        , one Douglas A-4 Skyhawk, one
        <a href="/wiki/Vought_F-8_Crusader" title="Vought F-8 Crusader">
         Vought F-8 Crusader
        </a>
        , one
        <a href="/wiki/Douglas_B-66_Destroyer" title="Douglas B-66 Destroyer">
         EB-66 Destroyer
        </a>
        and three unidentified types shot down by MiG-21s.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         1968
        </b>
        : U.S. claimed nine MiG-21s destroyed; North Vietnam claimed 17 US aircraft shot down by MiG-21s.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         1969
        </b>
        : U.S. destroyed three MiG-21s; one
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firebee" title="Firebee">
         Firebee
        </a>
        UAV destroyed by a MiG-21.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         1970
        </b>
        : U.S. destroyed two MiG-21s; North Vietnam claimed one F-4 Phantom and one CH-53 Sea Stallion helicopter shot down by MiG-21s.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         1972
        </b>
        : U.S. claimed 51 MiG-21s destroyed; North Vietnam claimed 53 US aircraft shot down by MiG-21s, including two B-52 Stratofortress bombers. Soviet General Fesenko, the main Soviet adviser to the North Vietnamese Air Force in 1972,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toperczer,_p._67_58-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Toperczer,_p._67-58">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
        recorded 34 MiG-21s destroyed in 1972.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toperczer,_p._67_58-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Toperczer,_p._67-58">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       During the Vietnam war, the VPAF claimed 103
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-4_Phantom" title="F-4 Phantom">
        F-4 Phantoms
       </a>
       were shot down by MiG-21s,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and they lost 60 MiG-21s in air combat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In late 2015, the VPAF announced that they would gradually phase out the MiG-21 in favor of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sukhoi_Su-17" title="Sukhoi Su-17">
        Su-22M4
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sukhoi_Su-27" title="Sukhoi Su-27">
        Su-27SK
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sukhoi_Su-30" title="Sukhoi Su-30">
        Su-30MK2V
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Egyptian.E2.80.93Syrian.E2.80.93Israeli_conflicts">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Egyptian–Syrian–Israeli_conflicts">
        Egyptian–Syrian–Israeli conflicts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Egyptian–Syrian–Israeli conflicts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MirageIIIShotDownByMiG-21.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="817" data-file-width="325" height="377" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/MirageIIIShotDownByMiG-21.jpg/150px-MirageIIIShotDownByMiG-21.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/MirageIIIShotDownByMiG-21.jpg/225px-MirageIIIShotDownByMiG-21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/MirageIIIShotDownByMiG-21.jpg/300px-MirageIIIShotDownByMiG-21.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MirageIIIShotDownByMiG-21.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Israeli
         <a href="/wiki/Dassault_Mirage_III" title="Dassault Mirage III">
          Mirage III
         </a>
         shot down by Egyptian MiG-21 during
         <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">
          October War
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The MiG-21 was also used extensively in the Middle East conflicts of the 1960s, 1970s and 1980s by the
       <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Air_Force" title="Egyptian Air Force">
        Egyptian Air Force
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Air_Force" title="Syrian Air Force">
        Syrian Air Force
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Air_Force" title="Iraqi Air Force">
        Iraqi Air Force
       </a>
       . The MiG-21 first encountered Israeli
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mirage_III" title="Mirage III">
        Mirage III
       </a>
       Cs on 14 November 1964, but it was not until 14 July 1966 that the first MiG-21 was shot down. Another six Syrian MiG-21s were shot down by Israeli Mirages on 7 April 1967. MiG-21s also faced
       <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_F-4_Phantom_II" title="McDonnell Douglas F-4 Phantom II">
        McDonnell Douglas F-4 Phantom IIs
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Douglas_A-4_Skyhawk" title="Douglas A-4 Skyhawk">
        Douglas A-4 Skyhawks
       </a>
       , but were later outclassed by the more modern
       <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_F-15_Eagle" title="McDonnell Douglas F-15 Eagle">
        McDonnell Douglas F-15 Eagle
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics_F-16_Fighting_Falcon" title="General Dynamics F-16 Fighting Falcon">
        General Dynamics F-16 Fighting Falcon
       </a>
       , which were acquired by Israel beginning in the mid-1970s.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the opening attacks of the 1967
       <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">
        Six-Day War
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Israeli_Air_Force" title="Israeli Air Force">
        Israeli Air Force
       </a>
       struck Arab air forces in four attack waves. In the first wave, IDF aircraft claimed to have destroyed eight Egyptian aircraft in air-to-air combat, of which seven were MiG-21s; Egypt claimed five kills scored by MiG-21PFs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During the second wave Israel claimed four MiG-21s downed in air-to-air combat, and the third wave resulted in two Syrian and one Iraqi MiG-21s claimed destroyed in the air. The fourth wave destroyed many more Syrian MiG-21s on the ground. Overall, Egypt lost around 100 out of about 110 MiG-21s they had, almost all on the ground; Syria lost 35 of 60 MiG-21F-13s and MiG-21PFs in the air and on the ground.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Between the end of the Six-Day War and the start of the
       <a href="/wiki/War_of_Attrition" title="War of Attrition">
        War of Attrition
       </a>
       , IDF Mirage fighters had six confirmed kills of Egyptian MiG-21s, in exchange for Egyptian MiG-21s scoring two confirmed and three probable kills against Israeli aircraft. During the War of Attrition itself, Israel claimed 56 confirmed kills
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       against Egyptian MiG-21s, while Egyptian MiG-21s claimed 14 confirmed and 12 probable kills against IDF aircraft. During this same time period, from the end of the Six Day War to the end of the War of Attrition, Israel claimed a total of 25 Syrian MiG-21s destroyed; the Syrians claimed three confirmed and four probable kills of Israel aircraft, although Israel denied these.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       High losses to Egyptian aircraft and continuous bombing during the War of Attrition caused Egypt to ask the Soviet Union for help. In June 1970, Soviet pilots and SAM crews arrived with their equipment. On 22 June 1970, a Soviet pilot flying a MiG-21MF shot down an Israeli A-4E. After some more successful intercepts by Soviet pilots and another Israeli A-4 being shot down on 25 July, Israel decided
       <a href="/wiki/Operation_Rimon_20" title="Operation Rimon 20">
        to plan an ambush
       </a>
       in response. On 30 July Israeli F-4s lured Soviet MiG-21s into an area where they were ambushed by Mirages.
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Asher_Snir&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Asher Snir (page does not exist)">
        Asher Snir
       </a>
       , flying a Mirage IIICJ, destroyed a Soviet MiG-21;
       <a href="/wiki/Avihu_Ben-Nun" title="Avihu Ben-Nun">
        Avihu Ben-Nun
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aviam_Sela" title="Aviam Sela">
        Aviam Sela
       </a>
       , both piloting F-4Es, each got a kill, and an unidentified pilot in another Mirage scored the fourth kill against the Soviet-flown MiG-21s. Three Soviet pilots were killed and the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       was alarmed by the losses. However, Soviet MiG-21 pilots and SAM crews destroyed a total of 2 Israeli aircraft, which helped to convince the Israelis to sign a ceasefire agreement.
      </p>
      <p>
       In September 1973, a large air battle erupted between Syria and Israel; Israel claimed a total of 12 Syrian MiG-21s destroyed, while Syria claimed eight kills scored by MiG-21s and admitted five losses.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the
       <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">
        Yom Kippur War
       </a>
       , Israel claimed 73 kills against Egyptian MiG-21s (65 confirmed). Egypt claimed 27 confirmed kills against Israeli aircraft by its MiG-21s, plus eight probables.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, according to most Israeli sources, these were exaggerated claims as Israeli air-to-air combat losses for the entire war did not exceed five to fifteen.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollack124_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pollack124-62">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On the Syrian front of the war, 6 October 1973 saw a flight of Syrian MiG-21MFs shoot down an IDF A-4E and a Mirage IIICJ while losing three of their own to Israeli
       <a href="/wiki/IAI_Nesher" title="IAI Nesher">
        IAI Neshers
       </a>
       . On 7 October, Syrian MiG-21MFs downed two Israeli F-4Es, three Mirage IIICJs and an A-4E while losing two of their MiGs to Neshers and one to an F-4E, plus two to friendly SAM fire. Iraqi MiG-21PFs also operated on this front, and on that same day destroyed two A-4Es while losing one MiG. On 8 October 1973, Syrian MiG-21PFMs downed three F-4Es, but six of their MiG-21s were lost. By the end of the war, Syrian MiG-21s claimed a total of 30 confirmed kills against Israeli aircraft; 29 MiG-21s were claimed (26 confirmed) as destroyed by the IDF.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Between the end of the Yom Kippur War and the start of the
       <a href="/wiki/1982_Lebanon_War" title="1982 Lebanon War">
        1982 Lebanon War
       </a>
       , Israel had received modern F-15s and F-16s, which were far superior to the old Syrian MiG-21MFs. According to the IDF, these new aircraft accounted for the destruction of 24 Syrian MiG-21s over this time period, though Syria did claim five kills against IDF aircraft with their MiG-21s armed with outdated K-13 missiles, although Israel denied they suffered any losses.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The 1982 Lebanon War started on 6 June 1982, and in the course of that war the IDF claimed to have destroyed about 45 Syrian MiG-21MFs. Syria claimed two confirmed and 15 probable kills of Israeli aircraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Two Israeli F-15 and one F-4 was damaged in combat with the MiG-21.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This air battle was the largest to occur since the Korean War.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Syrian_civil_war">
        Syrian civil war
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Syrian civil war">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_aviation_shootdowns_and_accidents_during_the_Syrian_Civil_War" title="List of aviation shootdowns and accidents during the Syrian Civil War">
        List of aviation shootdowns and accidents during the Syrian Civil War
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Starting in July 2012, after more than a year of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war" title="Syrian civil war">
        Syrian civil war
       </a>
       had seen no aerial action, the
       <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Air_Force" title="Syrian Air Force">
        Syrian Air Force
       </a>
       started operations against Syrian insurgents. MiG-21s were among the first combat ready aircraft used in bombings, rocket attacks and strafing runs with many videos recorded from the ground showing the jets in combat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The rebels had access to
       <a href="/wiki/Heavy_machine_gun" title="Heavy machine gun">
        heavy machine guns
       </a>
       , different antiaircraft guns and Chinese
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MANPADS" title="MANPADS">
        MANPADS
       </a>
       up to modern designs such as the
       <a href="/wiki/FN-6" title="FN-6">
        FN-6
       </a>
       . The first loss of a MiG-21 was recorded on 30 August 2012. Its registration was 2271. It was likely downed on take off or landing at
       <a href="/wiki/Abu_al-Duhur_Military_Airbase" title="Abu al-Duhur Military Airbase">
        Abu al-Duhur Military Airbase
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Abu_al-Duhur_Airbase" title="Siege of Abu al-Duhur Airbase">
        under siege by rebels
       </a>
       , by heavy machine gun fire.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A few days later a second MiG-21, registered 2280, was shot down and recorded on video on 4 September 2012. It was likely downed on take off or landing at Abu Dhuhur air base, under siege by rebels, by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KPV" title="KPV">
        KPV
       </a>
       14.5 mm machine gun fire.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 10 November 2014, a Syrian Air Force MiG-21bis, serialed 2204, was shot down by rebels either using a MANPADS or antiaircraft guns, near Sabboura town, 45 km east of Hama airbase where it was likely based. The pilot was killed. Video and picture evidence of the crash site surfaced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After four months, during which the Syrian Air Force suffered no losses to enemy fire, the last being a MiG-23, on 12 March 2016, a Syrian MiG-21 was shot down by
       <a href="/wiki/Jaysh_al-Nasr" title="Jaysh al-Nasr">
        Jaysh al-Nasr
       </a>
       over
       <a href="/wiki/Hama_Governorate" title="Hama Governorate">
        Hama
       </a>
       near
       <a href="/wiki/Kafr_Nabudah" title="Kafr Nabudah">
        Kafr Nabudah
       </a>
       . There were conflicting accounts of how it was brought down, the
       <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Observatory_for_Human_Rights" title="Syrian Observatory for Human Rights">
        Syrian Observatory for Human Rights
       </a>
       reported the warplane had been downed by two MANPADS, while Jaysh al-Nasr militants say they shot it down with anti-aircraft guns.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Video evidence suggesting it was a MANPADS. It seems the pilot ejected, but died, either killed by ground fire while descending or other causes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Middle_East_conflicts">
        Other Middle East conflicts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other Middle East conflicts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Egypt was shipped some American
       <a href="/wiki/AIM-9_Sidewinder" title="AIM-9 Sidewinder">
        Sidewinder
       </a>
       missiles, and these were fitted to their MiG-21s and successfully used in combat against Libyan Mirages and MiG-23s during the brief
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libyan-Egyptian_War" title="Libyan-Egyptian War">
        Libyan-Egyptian War
       </a>
       of July 1977.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        <b>
         Libya vs Egypt conflicts: MiG-21s in air-to-air combat
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-20">
         <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
          [1]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Date
        </th>
        <th>
         Aircraft scoring kill
        </th>
        <th>
         Victim
        </th>
       </tr>

         22 July 1977
        </th>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libyan_Air_Force_(1951-2011)" title="Libyan Air Force (1951-2011)">
          LARAF
         </a>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mirage_5" title="Mirage 5">
          Mirage 5
         </a>
         DE
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Air_Force" title="Egyptian Air Force">
          EAF
         </a>
         MiG-21MF
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         23 July 1977
        </th>
        <td>

        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         1979
        </th>
        <td>
         EAF MiG-21MF
        </td>
        <td>
         LARAF MiG-23MS
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h3>
       <span id="Iran.E2.80.93Iraq_War">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Iran–Iraq_War">
        Iran–Iraq War
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Iran–Iraq War">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       During the
       <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">
        Iran–Iraq War
       </a>
       , 23 Iraqi MiG-21s were shot down by Iranian F-14s, confirmed by the Iranian, Western and Iraqi sources
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 29 MiG-21s by F-4s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, from 1980 to 1988, the Iraqi MiG-21s shot down 43 Iranian fighter aircraft, against 49 MiG-21 losses in the same period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Libya">
        Libya
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Libya">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Libyan MiG-21s saw limited service during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_civil_war" title="2011 Libyan civil war">
        2011 Libyan civil war
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 15 March 2011, one MiG-21bis and one MiG-21UM flown by defector Libyan air force pilots who joined the rebellion, flew from Ghardabiya AB (near Sirte) and landed at Benina airport to become part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Free_Libyan_Air_Force" title="Free Libyan Air Force">
        Free Libyan Air Force
       </a>
       . On 17 March 2011 the MiG-21UM suffered a technical fault and crashed after takeoff from Benina airport.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 2 September 2014 a MiG-21 crashed in
       <a href="/wiki/Tobruk" title="Tobruk">
        Tobruk
       </a>
       due to pilot error during a pull up maneuver.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is unclear whether the pilot had been on a bombing mission in Derna, further East or had been performing an aerial ceremony for a pilot who died several days earlier.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       MiG-21s under the control of the Libyan
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Deputies" title="Council of Deputies">
        House of Representatives
       </a>
       have been used extensively to bomb forces loyal to the rival
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_General_National_Congress" title="New General National Congress">
        General National Congress
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Benghazi" title="Benghazi">
        Benghazi
       </a>
       during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_Libyan_Civil_War" title="2014 Libyan Civil War">
        2014 Libyan Civil War
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Horn_of_Africa">
        Horn of Africa
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Horn of Africa">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       During the
       <a href="/wiki/Ogaden_War" title="Ogaden War">
        Ogaden War
       </a>
       of 1977–78,
       <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Air_Force" title="Ethiopian Air Force">
        Ethiopian Air Force
       </a>
       F-5As engaged
       <a href="/wiki/Somali_Air_Force" title="Somali Air Force">
        Somali Air Force
       </a>
       MiG-21MFs in combat on several occasions. In one lopsided incident, two F-5As piloted by Israeli advisers or mercenaries engaged four MiG-21MFs. The MiGs were handled incompetently by the Somali pilots, and the F-5As destroyed two while the surviving pilots collided with each other avoiding an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIM-9" title="AIM-9">
        AIM-9
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ethiopia claimed to have shot down 10 Somali MiG-21MFs; while Somalia also claimed to have destroyed several Ethiopian MiG-21MFs, three F-5Es, one
       <a href="/wiki/English_Electric_Canberra" title="English Electric Canberra">
        Canberra
       </a>
       bomber and three
       <a href="/wiki/Douglas_DC-3" title="Douglas DC-3">
        Douglas DC-3s
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ethiopian MiG-21s were deployed largely in the ground attack role, and proved instrumental during the final offensive against Somali ground forces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Angola">
        Angola
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Angola">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SAAF-MirageF1CZv.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/SAAF-MirageF1CZv.JPG/220px-SAAF-MirageF1CZv.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/SAAF-MirageF1CZv.JPG/330px-SAAF-MirageF1CZv.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/SAAF-MirageF1CZv.JPG/440px-SAAF-MirageF1CZv.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SAAF-MirageF1CZv.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         MiG-21MF kill marking on a
         <a href="/wiki/South_African_Air_Force" title="South African Air Force">
          South African Air Force
         </a>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mirage_F1" title="Mirage F1">
          Mirage F1
         </a>
         CZ.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During
       <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">
        Angola
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Angolan_Civil_War" title="Angolan Civil War">
        long-running civil war
       </a>
       , MiG-21s of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban_Air_Force" title="Cuban Air Force">
        Cuban Air Force
       </a>
       were frequently deployed to attack ground targets manned by rebel forces or engage
       <a href="/wiki/South_African_Air_Force" title="South African Air Force">
        South African Air Force
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mirage_F1" title="Mirage F1">
        Mirage F1s
       </a>
       conducting cross-border strikes. Most MiG-21 losses over Angola were attributed to accurate ground fire, such as an example downed by
       <a href="/wiki/UNITA" title="UNITA">
        National Union for the Total Independence of Angola
       </a>
       (UNITA) insurgents near
       <a href="/wiki/Luena,_Moxico_Province" title="Luena, Moxico Province">
        Luena
       </a>
       with an American
       <a href="/wiki/FIM-92_Stinger" title="FIM-92 Stinger">
        FIM-92 Stinger
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite extensive losses to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man-portable_air-defense_systems" title="Man-portable air-defense systems">
        man-portable air-defense systems
       </a>
       , MiG-21s were instrumental during the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cuito_Cuanavale" title="Battle of Cuito Cuanavale">
        Battle of Cuito Cuanavale
       </a>
       ; Cuban pilots became accustomed to flying up to three sorties a day. Both the MiG-21MF and the MiG-21bis were deployed almost exclusively in the
       <a href="/wiki/Fighter-bomber" title="Fighter-bomber">
        fighter/bomber
       </a>
       role. As interceptors they were somewhat unsuccessful due to their inability to detect low-flying South African aircraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Angola_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Angola-84">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On November 6, 1981, a Mirage F1CZ achieved South Africa's first confirmed air-to-air kill since the
       <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">
        Korean War
       </a>
       when it destroyed Cuban Lieutenant Danacio Valdez's MiG-21MF with 30mm cannon fire.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transport_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Transport-85">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 5 October 1982, Mirages escorting an
       <a href="/wiki/English_Electric_Canberra" title="English Electric Canberra">
        English Electric Canberra
       </a>
       on routine reconnaissance over
       <a href="/wiki/Cahama" title="Cahama">
        Cahama
       </a>
       were engaged by at least two MiG-21bis. A South African radar operator picked up the attacking MiGs and was able to alert the Mirage pilots in advance, instructing them to change course immediately. As they jettisoned their auxiliary tanks, however, they were pinpointed by the Cubans, who opened pursuit. In a vicious
       <a href="/wiki/Dogfight" title="Dogfight">
        dogfight
       </a>
       , SAAF Major Johann Rankin closed range and maneuvered into the MiGs' rear cones. From there, one of his two
       <a href="/wiki/R.550_Magic" title="R.550 Magic">
        R.550 Magic
       </a>
       missiles impacted directly behind the lead MiG and forced it down. The second aircraft, piloted by a Lieutenant Raciel Marrero Rodriguez, could not detect the Mirage's proximity until it had entered his turn radius and was perforated by Rankin's autocannon. This damaged MiG-21 landed safely at
       <a href="/wiki/Lubango" title="Lubango">
        Lubango
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transport_85-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Transport-85">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Contacts between MiG-21s and SAAF Mirage F1s or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mirage_III" title="Mirage III">
        Mirage IIIs
       </a>
       became increasingly common throughout the 1980s. Between 1984 and 1988, thirteen MiG-21s were lost over Angola.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 9 August 1984, a particularly catastrophic accident occurred when the 9th Fighter Training Squadrons and the 12th Fighter Squadrons of the Cuban Air Force attempted to carry out an exercise in poor weather. A single MiG-21bis and three MiG-23s were lost.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Angola_84-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Angola-84">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 14 December 1988, an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angolan_Air_Force" title="Angolan Air Force">
        Angolan Air Force
       </a>
       MiG-21bis strayed off course and executed an emergency landing in
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">
        Namibia
       </a>
       , where it was seized by local authorities. Since Angola did not request its return after the
       <a href="/wiki/South_African_Border_War" title="South African Border War">
        South African Border War
       </a>
       , the MiG was restored by
       <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Aircraft_Corporation" title="Atlas Aircraft Corporation">
        Atlas Aviation
       </a>
       and currently resides at
       <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Base_Swartkop" title="Air Force Base Swartkop">
        Swartkops Air Force Base
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">
        Pretoria
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Congo">
        Congo
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Congo">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       MiG-21s, along with its Chinese copy (the
       <a href="/wiki/Chengdu_J-7" title="Chengdu J-7">
        F-7 Skybolt
       </a>
       ), flew ground sorties during the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Congo_War" title="First Congo War">
        First
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Congo_War" title="Second Congo War">
        Second Congo Wars
       </a>
       , sometimes being piloted by mercenaries.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Yugoslavia">
        Yugoslavia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Yugoslavia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BAM-19a-MiG_21_F-13.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/BAM-19a-MiG_21_F-13.jpg/220px-BAM-19a-MiG_21_F-13.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/BAM-19a-MiG_21_F-13.jpg/330px-BAM-19a-MiG_21_F-13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/BAM-19a-MiG_21_F-13.jpg/440px-BAM-19a-MiG_21_F-13.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BAM-19a-MiG_21_F-13.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Yugoslavian Air Force MiG-21F-13
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">
        Yugoslavia
       </a>
       purchased its first batch of MiG-21s in 1962 from the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       . In the period from 1962 to the early 1980s Yugoslavia had purchased 261 MiG-21s in ten variants. There was 41 MiG-21f-13, 36 MiG-21PfM, 25 MiG-21M, 6 MiG-21MF, 46 MiG-21bis, 45 MiG-21bisK, 12 MiG-21R, 18 MiG-21U, 25 MiG-21UM and 7 MiG-21US.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From 1964 to 1992, about 80 aircraft had been lost in accidents.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2008)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Yugoslav Air force units that operated MiG-21 were the 204th fighter-aviation regiment at
       <a href="/wiki/Batajnica_Air_Base" title="Batajnica Air Base">
        Batajnica Air Base
       </a>
       (126th, 127th and 128th fighter-aviation squadrons), 117th fighter-aviation regiment at
       <a href="/wiki/%C5%BDeljava_Air_Base" title="Željava Air Base">
        Željava Air Base
       </a>
       (124th and 125th fighter-aviation squadron and 352nd recon squadron), 83rd fighter-aviation regiment at
       <a href="/wiki/Slatina_Air_Base" title="Slatina Air Base">
        Slatina Air Base
       </a>
       (123rd and 130th fighter aviation squadron), 185th fighter-bomber-aviation squadron (129th fighter-aviation squadron) at Pula and 129th training center at Batajnica air base.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the early stages of the 1991–1995
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yugoslav_wars" title="Yugoslav wars">
        Yugoslav wars
       </a>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_People%27s_Army" title="Yugoslav People's Army">
        Yugoslav People's Army
       </a>
       used MiG-21s in a ground-attack role, while Croatian and Slovenian forces did not have air forces at the beginning of the war. Aircraft from air bases in Slovenia, Croatia and Bosnia and Herzegovina were relocated to air bases in Serbia. Detailed records show at least seven MiG-21s were shot down by AA defenses in
       <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">
        Croatia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">
        Bosnia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avijacija_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Avijacija-89">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A MiG-21
       <a href="/wiki/1992_European_Community_Monitor_Mission_helicopter_downing" title="1992 European Community Monitor Mission helicopter downing">
        shot down an EC helicopter in 1992
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Croatia acquired three MiG-21s in 1992 through defections by Croatian pilots serving with the
       <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_People%27s_Army" title="Yugoslav People's Army">
        JNA
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       two of which were lost in subsequent actions – one to Serbian air defenses, the other in a
       <a href="/wiki/Friendly_fire" title="Friendly fire">
        friendly fire
       </a>
       accident.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACIG_MiGs_Over_Croatia_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ACIG_MiGs_Over_Croatia-92">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1993, Croatia purchased about 40 MiG-21s in violation of an arms embargo,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACIG_MiGs_Over_Croatia_92-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ACIG_MiGs_Over_Croatia-92">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but only about 20 of these entered service, while the rest were used for spare parts. Croatia used them alongside the sole remaining defector for ground attack missions in operations
       <a href="/wiki/Operation_Flash" title="Operation Flash">
        Flash
       </a>
       (during which one was lost) and
       <a href="/wiki/Operation_Storm" title="Operation Storm">
        Storm
       </a>
       . The only air-to-air action for Croatian MiGs was an attempt by two of them to intercept
       <a href="/wiki/Soko_J-22_Orao" title="Soko J-22 Orao">
        Soko J-22 Oraos
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Republika_Srpska_Air_Force" title="Republika Srpska Air Force">
        Republika Srpska Air Force
       </a>
       on ground attack mission on August 7, 1995. After some maneuvering, both sides disengaged without firing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACIG_MiGs_Over_Croatia_92-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ACIG_MiGs_Over_Croatia-92">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Remaining Yugoslav MiG-21s were flown to Serbia by 1992 and continued their service in the newly created Federal Republic of Yugoslavia. During the 1999
       <a href="/wiki/NATO_bombing_of_Yugoslavia" title="NATO bombing of Yugoslavia">
        NATO bombing of Yugoslavia
       </a>
       , 3 MiG-21s were destroyed on the ground.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avijacija_89-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Avijacija-89">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Romania">
        Romania
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Romania">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MiG-21_Lancer_C_firing_rockets.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="778" data-file-width="1166" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MiG-21_Lancer_C_firing_rockets.jpg/220px-MiG-21_Lancer_C_firing_rockets.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MiG-21_Lancer_C_firing_rockets.jpg/330px-MiG-21_Lancer_C_firing_rockets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MiG-21_Lancer_C_firing_rockets.jpg/440px-MiG-21_Lancer_C_firing_rockets.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MiG-21_Lancer_C_firing_rockets.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A Romanian MiG-21 LanceR C during a training exercise.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1962,
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Air_Force" title="Romanian Air Force">
        Romanian Air Force
       </a>
       (RoAF) received first 12 MiG-21F-13, followed by another 12 of the same variant in 1963. Deliveries continued over the next years with other variants: 38 aircraft of MiG-21RFM (PF) variant in 1965, 7 MiG-21U-400/600 in 1965–1968, 56 MiG-21RFMM (PFM) in 1966–1968, 12 MiG-21R in 1968–1972, 68 MiG-21M plus 11 MiG-21US in 1969–1970, 74 MiG-21MF/MF-75 in 1972–1975, and 27 MiG-21UM in 1972–1980 plus another 5 of the same variant in 1990, for a total number of 322 aircraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aa_93-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-aa-93">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Beginning in 1993, Russia did not offer spare parts for the MiG-23 and MiG-29 for the RoAF. Initially, this was the context for the modernization of the Romanian MiG-21s with
       <a href="/wiki/Elbit_Systems" title="Elbit Systems">
        Elbit Systems
       </a>
       , and because it was easier to maintain these fighter jets. In 1995–2002, a total of 111 MiG-21s were modernized, of which 71 were M and MF/MF-75 variants modernized under the LanceR A designation (for ground attack), 14 were UM variant as LanceR B designation (trainer), and another 26 MF/MF-75 variant were modernized under LanceR C designation (air superiority).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aa_93-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-aa-93">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, only 36 LanceRs are operational for the RoAF. It can use both Western and Eastern armament such as the R-60M, R-73, Magic 2, or Python III missiles.
      </p>
      <p>
       They will be replaced by a squadron of 12 F-16AM/BM fighters by 2020 with the first aircraft arriving in the second half of 2016. Another squadron will be purchased with more newer versions of the F-16 or other types of multirole plane jets like : Dassault Rafale-B/C/M, used McDonnell-Douglas F/A-18C/D Hornet, Boeing F/A-18E/F Super Hornet or Eurofighter Typhoon, to complete the minimum number of 48 multirole fighters, required in 2004 by NATO when Romania joined.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite being one of the newest MiG-21 fleets in service, the Romanian MiG-21 LanceR fleet was grounded due to difficulties maintaining the aircraft, and since 1996 it has had an accident rate of over 30 per 100,000 hours. Serviceability rates below 50% are not uncommon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Bulgaria">
        Bulgaria
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Bulgaria">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bulgarian_Air_Force_Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21bis_Lofting-4.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="849" data-file-width="1280" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bulgarian_Air_Force_Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21bis_Lofting-4.jpg/220px-Bulgarian_Air_Force_Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21bis_Lofting-4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bulgarian_Air_Force_Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21bis_Lofting-4.jpg/330px-Bulgarian_Air_Force_Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21bis_Lofting-4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bulgarian_Air_Force_Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21bis_Lofting-4.jpg/440px-Bulgarian_Air_Force_Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21bis_Lofting-4.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bulgarian_Air_Force_Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21bis_Lofting-4.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Air_Force" title="Bulgarian Air Force">
          Bulgarian Air Force
         </a>
         MiG-21bis
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Air_Force" title="Bulgarian Air Force">
        Bulgarian Air Force
       </a>
       received a total of 224 MiG-21 aircraft. From September 1963 the 19th Fighter Regiment of the Air Force received 12 MiG-21F-13s. Later some of these aircraft were converted for reconnaissance as MiG-21F-13Rs, which were submitted to the 26th Reconnaissance Regiment in 1988. In January 1965 the 18th Fighter Regiment received a squadron of 12 MiG-21PFs, some of which also were converted and used as a reconnaissance aircraft (MiG-oboznachevnieto 21PFR). The 26 Regiment reconnaissance aircraft from this squadron were removed from service in 1991, the 15 Fighter Regiment in 1965 received another 12 MiG-21PF fighters and in 1977–1978 operated another 36 refurbished aircraft. This unit received two more aircraft in 1984 and operated them until 1992.
      </p>
      <p>
       For reconnaissance, a regiment received 26 specialized reconnaissance MiG-21Rs in 1962, and in 1969–1970, 19 Fighter Aviation Regiment received 15 MiG-21m aircraft, which operated in 21 Fighter Aviation Regiment and were removed from active service in 1990. An additional 12 MiG-21MF fighters were received in 1974–1975, with a reconnaissance version of the MiG-21MFR provided to the 26th Reconnaissance Regiment and eksloatirani until 2000, when removed from active service.
      </p>
      <p>
       From 1983 to 1990, the Bulgaria Air Force received 72 MiG-21bis. Of these, 30 (six new and renovated) are under option with ACS and provided to the 19th Fighter Regiment; the rest are equipped with the "Lazur". This batch was taken out of service in 2000.
      </p>
      <p>
       Besides fighters, the Air Force has received 39 MiG-21U trainers (one in 1966), five MiG-21US in 1969–1970 and 27 MiG-21UM (new) during 1974–1980, another six refurbished ex-Soviet examples in 1990. In 1982, three MiG-21UM trainers were sold to Cambodia and in 1994 another 10 examples. MiG-21UMs were also sold to India. Oher training aircraft were removed from active service in 2000. A total of 38 aircraft were lost in the period 1963–2000.
      </p>
      <p>
       The last flight of a
       <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Air_Force" title="Bulgarian Air Force">
        Bulgarian Air Force
       </a>
       MiG-21 took off from
       <a href="/wiki/Graf_Ignatievo_Air_Base" title="Graf Ignatievo Air Base">
        Graf Ignatievo Air Base
       </a>
       on 31 December 2015. On 18 December 2015, there was an official ceremony for the retirement of the type of the active duty. Aircraft have been put in "active" storage.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Known_MiG-21_aces">
        Known MiG-21 aces
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Known MiG-21 aces">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MiG-21_in_US_service.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1220" data-file-width="1800" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/MiG-21_in_US_service.jpg/220px-MiG-21_in_US_service.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/MiG-21_in_US_service.jpg/330px-MiG-21_in_US_service.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/MiG-21_in_US_service.jpg/440px-MiG-21_in_US_service.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MiG-21_in_US_service.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         MiG-21 with American markings used for training of American pilots in flight
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Several pilots have attained
       <a href="/wiki/Flying_ace" title="Flying ace">
        ace status
       </a>
       (five or more aerial victories/kills) while flying the MiG-21.
       <a href="/wiki/Nguy%E1%BB%85n_V%C4%83n_C%E1%BB%91c" title="Nguyễn Văn Cốc">
        Nguyễn Văn Cốc
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_People%27s_Air_Force" title="Vietnam People's Air Force">
        Vietnam People's Air Force
       </a>
       (VPAF; also referred to as the NVAF), who scored nine kills in MiG-21s is regarded as the most successful.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NV_Aces_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NV_Aces-97">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Twelve other VPAF pilots were credited with five or more aerial victories while flying the MiG-21:
       <a href="/wiki/Ph%E1%BA%A1m_Thanh_Ng%C3%A2n" title="Phạm Thanh Ngân">
        Phạm Thanh Ngân
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Nguy%E1%BB%85n_H%E1%BB%93ng_Nh%E1%BB%8B" title="Nguyễn Hồng Nhị">
        Nguyễn Hồng Nhị
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mai_V%C4%83n_C%C6%B0%E1%BB%9Dng" title="Mai Văn Cường">
        Mai Văn Cường
       </a>
       (both eight kills);
       <a href="/wiki/%C4%90%E1%BA%B7ng_Ng%E1%BB%8Dc_Ng%E1%BB%B1" title="Đặng Ngọc Ngự">
        Đặng Ngọc Ngự
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (seven kills),
       <a href="/wiki/V%C5%A9_Ng%E1%BB%8Dc_%C4%90%E1%BB%89nh" title="Vũ Ngọc Đỉnh">
        Vũ Ngọc Đỉnh
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Nguy%E1%BB%85n_Ng%E1%BB%8Dc_%C4%90%E1%BB%99" title="Nguyễn Ngọc Độ">
        Nguyễn Ngọc Độ
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Nguy%E1%BB%85n_Nh%E1%BA%ADt_Chi%C3%AAu" title="Nguyễn Nhật Chiêu">
        Nguyễn Nhật Chiêu
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/L%C3%AA_Thanh_%C4%90%E1%BA%A1o" title="Lê Thanh Đạo">
        Lê Thanh Đạo
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Nguy%E1%BB%85n_%C4%90%C4%83ng_K%E1%BB%89nh" title="Nguyễn Đăng Kỉnh">
        Nguyễn Đăng Kỉnh
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Nguy%E1%BB%85n_%C4%90%E1%BB%A9c_So%C3%A1t" title="Nguyễn Đức Soát">
        Nguyễn Đức Soát
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Nguy%E1%BB%85n_Ti%E1%BA%BFn_S%C3%A2m" title="Nguyễn Tiến Sâm">
        Nguyễn Tiến Sâm
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (six kills each), and
       <a href="/wiki/Nguy%E1%BB%85n_V%C4%83n_Ngh%C4%A9a" title="Nguyễn Văn Nghĩa">
        Nguyễn Văn Nghĩa
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_2008_1-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_2008-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (five kills).
      </p>
      <p>
       Additionally, three
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian" title="Syrian">
        Syrian
       </a>
       pilots are known to have attained ace status while flying the MiG-21. Syrian airmen: M. Mansour
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACIG_Syria_98-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ACIG_Syria-98">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       recorded five solo kills (with one additional probable), B. Hamshu
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACIG_Syria_98-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ACIG_Syria-98">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       scored five solo kills, and A. el-Gar
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACIG_Syria_98-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ACIG_Syria-98">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       tallied four solo and one shared kill, all three during the 1973–1974 engagements against Israel.
      </p>
      <p>
       Due to the incomplete nature of available records, there are several pilots who have un-confirmed aerial victories (probable kills), which when confirmed would award them "Ace" Status: S. A. Razak
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACIG_Iraq_99-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ACIG_Iraq-99">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of the Iraqi Air Force with four known kills scored during the
       <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">
        Iran–Iraq War
       </a>
       (until 1991; sometimes referred to as the Persian Gulf War), A. Wafai
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACIG_Egypt_100-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ACIG_Egypt-100">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of the Egyptian Air Force with four known kills against Israel.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        For specific information on kills scored by and against MiG-21s sorted by country see the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-21_operators" title="Mikoyan-Gurevich MiG-21 operators">
         Mikoyan-Gurevich MiG-21 operators
        </a>
        page.
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>

          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Air_Forces_2015_pg._11_102-0">

       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Croatia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Croatia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Croatia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Croatia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Croatia.svg/46px-Flag_of_Croatia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">
         Croatia
        </a>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Croatian_Air_Force" title="Croatian Air Force">
           Croatian Air Force
          </a>
          : 8 MiG-21BisD's and 4 MiG-21UMD's in service in 2015. Upgraded in 2003 to MiG-21BisD/UMD standard and refurbished again 2014. They will remain in active use until 2019/2020 when they will be replaced by a new 4th generation fighter.
         </li>
        </ul>

        </a>
        . Other than MiG-21F13 supplied by Soviet Union, China also traded a small amount of MiG-21MFs with J-7 export variations, then developed J-7C/D based on MiG-21MFs. The deal was between China and a certain Middle Eastern country.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AirForceWorld.com_China_Mig-21MF_Trade_121-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-AirForceWorld.com_China_Mig-21MF_Trade-121">
          [118]
         </a>
        </sup>
        In May 2013, Official publication from Chengdu Aircraft Corp reported J-7 production has been ceased after decades of manufacturing variations of this Chinese made MiG-21.

         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Flag_of_Slovakia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Slovakia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Flag_of_Slovakia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Slovakia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Flag_of_Slovakia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Slovakia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">
         Slovakia
        </a>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Slovak_Air_Force" title="Slovak Air Force">
           Slovak Air Force
          </a>
          In 1993, after the dissolution of Czechoslovakia, Slovak Air Force obtained 13 MiG-21MAs, 36 MiG-21MFs, eight MiG-21Rs, two MiG-21USs and 11 MiG-21UMs. They were withdrawn in 2003. Some of them are in museums across the country, some are stored and will be destroyed.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
           <a href="#cite_note-133">
            [130]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
